مسائل: البناء على أرض الوقف والغراس عليها كثير الوقوع في البلدان خصوصاً في دمشق فإن بساتينها كثيرة وأكثرها أراضي أوقاف غرس عليها المستأجرون وجعلوها أملاكاً وأكثر إجاراتها بأقل من أجر المثل إما ابتداء وإما بزيادة الرغبات وكذلك حوانيت البلدان، فإذا طلب المتولي أو القاضي رفع إجاراتها إلى أجر المثل يتظلم سكانها ومستأجروها ويزعمون أنه ظلم عليهم وهم ظالمون، وبعض الصدور والأكابر أيضاً قد يعاونونهم ويزعمون أن هذا تحريك فتنة فيجب على كل قاض عادل عالم وكل قيم أمين غير ظالم أن ينظر ؛ فإن كان بحيث إذا رفع المستأجر بناءه وغرسه لا يستأجره الناس بأكثر فليبقها، وإذا كان بحيث لو رفع وتبقى الأرض بيضاء نقية يستأجرها المستأجرون بأكثر بزيادة لا يتغابن فيها الناس وثبت هذا بخبر اثنين خبيرين نقول لصاحب البناء إما أن تفسخ وترفع البناء والغراس أو تقبلها بهذه الإجارة فإن قبلها تبقى الإجارة عليه وإلا يرفع بناءه وغرسه وقلما يضر رفعه بالأرض فلا يبالي به وإن ضربها ضرراً بيناً بإذن القاضي للمستأجر برفع بنائه صيانة للوقف عن الضرر فيأمر المتولي بتملكه مقلوعاً إن رضي صاحب البناء وإلا فيؤجر المتولي الأرض من الغير ويبقى الباني إلى أن يتخلص ملكه، ولا يكون ذلك مانعاً من الإجارة لأنه لا بد للباني عليه حتى لا يملك رفعه فكأنها غير مشغولة؛ وهكذا قالوا ولكن من يستأجر الأرض مع بناء الحانوت فيها إذ لا يمكنه التمتع فيها فالوجه أن يرضى بضرر القلع ويؤمر به وهو يسير غالباً فيؤخذ البناء غير مقلوع بقيمته مقلوعاً ويحصل للوقف غبطة عظيمة، هذا كله إذا كان بدون أجر المثل ابتداء أو الآن. وإلا فلا تفسخ بزيادة أحد وإن زاد ضعف الأجرة إلا أن تنقضي مدة الإجارة فيعطيها للطالب بالزيادة، أما إذا زاد صرح به العلامة عالماً بذلك. وذكر الخصاف أن الواقف أيضاً إذا أجر بالأقل مما لا يتغابن الناس في مثله فإنها غير جائزة ويبطلها القاضي فإن كان الواقف مأموناً وفعل ذلك على طريق السهو والغفلة أقره القاضي في يده وأمره بإجارتها بالأصلح، وإن كان غير مأمون أخرجها من يده وجعلها في يد من يثق بدينه وكذا إذا أجرها الواقف سنين كثيرة ممن يخاف أن تتلف في يده قال : يبطل القاضي الإجارة ويخرجها من يد المستأجر .اهـ. فإذا كان هذا في الواقف فالمتولي أولى . وفي الإسعاف : لو شرط الواقف أن لا يؤجر المتولي الوقف ولا شيئاً منه أو أن لا يدفعه مزارعة أو على أن لا يعمل على ما فيه من الأشجار أو شرط أن لا يؤجر إلا ثلاث سنين ثم لا يعقد عليه إلا بعد انقضاء العقد الأول كان شرطه معتبراً ولا تجوز مخالفته اهـ. وسيأتي في بيان الشروط ما لا يعتبر منها إن شاء الله تعالى وسيأتي في كتاب الإجارات بيان مدتها في الأوقاف وحكم الإجارة الطويلة إن شاء الله تعالى. وذكر الخصاف أنه لو تبين أن المستأجر يخاف منه على رقبة الوقف يفسخ القاضي الإجارة ويخرجه من يده ولو كان المستأجر أمين القاضي.
The waqf (is taken) forcibly from its owner if the land is diminished by removal; otherwise, his consent is necessary. Thus mentioned the generality of the commentators, among them our master the author of al-Bahr, so one should rely on what is in the commentaries written for transmitting the madhhab, contrary to the transmissions of fatwas, and Allah knows best. Regarding his statement 'and the apparent is that it does not accept the increase', al-Ramli said: The apparent is contrary to this apparent, and it is to liken it to monthly lease, so when the year ends, the trustee may rescind the lease, for there is no difference between them in terms of conclusion likewise; he only did not mention it, relying on the first, because one knows its ruling. The conclusion is that it does not accept the increase in all cases where the equivalent rent has not increased in itself, due to the binding nature of the contract and absence of cause for rescission. The apparent is that he meant by his statement 'and the apparent' that it is like monthly lease in not accepting increase, for in monthly lease it does not accept but one waits until the month ends, and by this his statement is correct. But if he had said 'and he did not mention the annual lease, and the apparent is that it is likewise', it would be shorter and better. I say: This understanding is far from the author's words; rather, the apparent from his words is the distinction between them, and that in the annual lease it is not taken from his hand even if the year is completed, due to his proof 'because the increase...'; and it is objected that there is then no difference between monthly and annual. In the treatise of the scholar Qunali Zada: Issues: Building on waqf land and planting trees on it occur often in cities, especially in Damascus, for its orchards are many and most of them are waqf lands on which lessees planted and made them properties, and most of their leases are for less than the equivalent rent, either initially or by increase of desires, and likewise the shops of cities. So when the trustee or judge demands raising their leases to the equivalent rent, their residents and lessees complain and claim that it is injustice to them, while they are unjust. And some of the notables and greats may also assist them, claiming that this is stirring up fitna. So it is obligatory upon every just and knowledgeable judge and every trustworthy and non-oppressive trustee to consider: if when the lessee removes his building and trees, people will not rent it for more, then let him leave it. And if when he removes and the land remains bare and clean, lessees will rent it for more, an increase that people would not overlook, and this is proven by the report of two expert witnesses, then we say to the owner of the building: either rescind and remove the building and trees, or accept this lease. If he accepts, the lease remains upon him; otherwise, he removes his building and trees, and rarely does removal harm the land, so he should not be heeded. And if it causes clear harm, the judge permits the lessee to remove his building to protect the waqf from harm, and orders the trustee to take possession of it uprooted if the building owner agrees; otherwise, the trustee leases the land to someone else and the builder remains until his ownership is freed, and that does not prevent the lease because the builder is compelled to leave, so the land is as if unoccupied. Thus they said. But who will lease land with a shop built in it, since he cannot enjoy it? So the proper approach is to tolerate the harm of removal and he is ordered to do so, and it is usually slight, so the building is taken not uprooted with its value as uprooted, and a great benefit accrues to the waqf. All this if (the lease) is without the equivalent rent initially or now. Otherwise, it is not rescinded by anyone's increase, even if he doubles the rent, unless the lease term expires, then it is given to the requester with the increase. But if it increases, the scholar explicitly stated that. And al-Khassaf mentioned that the waqif also, if he leases for less than what people would not overlook in its like, then it is not permissible and the judge voids it. If the waqif is trustworthy and did it out of inadvertence and negligence, the judge confirms him and orders him to lease for the most beneficial; if he is not trustworthy, he removes him and puts in charge someone whose religion he trusts. Likewise, if the waqif leases for many years to someone feared to cause loss in his hands, he said: the judge voids the lease and removes it from the lessee's hand. So if this is (the ruling) for the waqif, then the trustee is even more so. In al-Is'af: If the waqif stipulates that the trustee should not lease the waqf or any part of it, or not enter into a sharecropping contract, or not work on its trees, or stipulates that he should only lease for three years and then not contract again until after the first contract ends, his stipulation is considered valid and may not be violated. The inadmissible conditions will be discussed later, God willing. And the limit of leases in waqf and the ruling on long leases will come in the Book of Leases, God willing. And al-Khassaf mentioned that if it becomes evident that the lessee is feared for the corpus of the waqf, the judge rescinds the lease and removes him from his hand, even if the lessee is the judge's trustee.
Source pages p. 399

Commentary
This passage from Al-Bahr al-Raiq contains multiple interrelated rulings concerning buildings and trees on waqf land. It begins with a principle: if removing a structure damages the land (e.g., by excavation), the waqf may force the owner to sell the structure to the waqf; otherwise, the owner's consent is needed. The author then engages in a debate with al-Ramli about whether a long-term lease can be increased during its term. The author distinguishes between monthly and annual leases, while al-Ramli equates them. The core discussion is about the judge's and trustee's role in adjusting rents to ujr al-mithl, especially in cases where lessees have built on waqf land. The text cites a fatwa from 'Allama Qunali Zada detailing the problem in Damascus: many orchards are waqf, lessees have planted trees and built, and pay below-market rent. When the judge or trustee tries to raise rent, lessees object. The fatwa provides a detailed procedure: the judge must determine whether, after removing the building/trees, the land would command a market rent. If not, the lease remains; if yes, the lessee must either accept the increased rent or remove his improvements. If removal harms the land, the judge orders the trustee to purchase the building at its 'uprooted value' (i.e., as salvaged materials). The text also includes rulings from al-Khassaf about the waqif's own leases: if he leases below market, the judge may remove him if untrustworthy. Finally, it mentions the applicability of waqif's stipulations regarding leasing and the judge's power to rescind if the lessee is a risk to the waqf corpus. The overall theme is protecting the waqf's income and corpus.

Key Terms
رقبة الوقف (raqabat al-waqf): The corpus (capital) of the waqf, the physical asset that must be preserved.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Khassaf, what may the judge do if it becomes evident that a lessee is feared to harm the waqf corpus?
A) The judge must wait until the lease expires.
B) The judge may rescind the lease and remove the lessee.
C) The judge may only warn the lessee.
D) The judge must consult the waqif.

Key Takeaway
The waqf's corpus is paramount: waqif's stipulations bind trustees, and judges can rescind leases to protect the asset from risky lessees.

Trustee's Liability for Leasing Below Market Rent
Ruling on trustee's liability for deficient lease

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that a trustee is not personally liable when he leases waqf property below ujr al-mithl, and identify the obligation of the lessee to pay the equivalent rent.

Core Idea
A trustee who leases below market is not liable; the lessee must pay ujr al-mithl.

This lesson addresses a common concern in waqf management: if a trustee leases property below the going rate, is he personally liable for the shortfall? The Hanafi position, as clarified by later fatwa authorities, is clear: the trustee is not a guarantor of the market rent. Instead, the lessee must pay the equivalent rent (ujr al-mithl) for the period of use. We will examine the text from Al-Bahr al-Raiq and the supporting analogy from Jami' al-Fusulayn.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal actors
Distinguish the trustee (متولي) who manages the waqf and the lessee (مستأجر) who uses the property. Determine who bears the risk of a deficient lease.
2
Classify the contract
The lease is fasid (corrupt) due to a gross deficiency in rent. Understand the Hanafi distinction: fasid is invalid but obligates the lessee to pay ujr al-mithl for usage.
3
Extract the ruling
From the text: the trustee is not liable (لا ضمان عليه). The lessee must pay ujr al-mithl. Note the rejection of the opposite view as a mistake.
4
Analyze the evidence
The ruling relies on Qasim's fatwa and an analogy from Jami' al-Fusulayn: selling orphan's property to a lower but reliable bidder is permissible, and similarly leasing to a reliable person below market.
5
Apply the principle
Hypothetical: trustee leases a shop for 800/month when market is 1000. The lessee must pay 1000; trustee owes nothing.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم اعلم أن المتولي إذا آجر بأقل من أجرة المثل بنقصان فاحش حتى فسدت لا ضمان عليه وإنما يلزم المستأجر أجرة المثل، وقد توهم بعض من لا خبرة له ولا دربة أنه يكون ضامناً ما نقص وهو غلط ؛ قاسم في فتاواه مستنداً إلى النقول الصريحة. وفي جامع الفصولين ولو استباع مال اليتيم بألف وآخر بألف ومائة والأول أملاً يبيعه الوصي من الأول، وكذا الإجارة تؤجر بثمانية للإملاء لا بعشرة لغيره وكذا متولي الوقف اهـ.
Then know that if the trustee leases for less than the equivalent rent with a gross deficiency (exorbitant decrease) such that (the contract) is corrupt (fasid), there is no liability upon him; rather, the lessee is obliged to pay the equivalent rent. And some who have no experience or training have imagined that he is liable for the deficiency, and it is a mistake. Qasim in his fatwas, relying on explicit transmissions (concluded this). And in Jami' al-Fusulayn: 'If (the trustee) sells the property of an orphan for one thousand and another offers one thousand one hundred, but the first is reliable, the executor sells to the first. Likewise, the lease: he leases for eight for a reliable person, not for ten to another, and likewise the trustee of the waqf.'
Source pages p. 400

Commentary
This short passage from Al-Bahr al-Raiq addresses a common misunderstanding about the trustee's liability when he leases waqf property below market rate. The author, Ibn Najim, clarifies that even if the trustee enters into a corrupt (fasid) lease due to a gross deficiency in rent, he is not personally liable for the shortfall. Instead, the lessee (musta'jir) is obligated to pay the full ujr al-mithl (equivalent rent) for the period of use. The trustee is not a guarantor of the difference. The author refutes those who mistakenly think the trustee owes the amount he 'lost' for the waqf. He supports this with a reference to Qasim's fatwa and an analogy from Jami' al-Fusulayn: a guardian for an orphan may sell to a lower bidder if that bidder is trustworthy, and similarly a trustee may lease to a reliable person at a lower rate if there is a valid reason (like trustworthiness). However, the key point is that the trustee does not become liable; the lessee owes the fair market rent. This ruling applies specifically when the lease is corrupted (fasid) due to the deficiency; if the lease is valid (sahih) but below market, the lessee still owes ujr al-mithl based on the earlier ruling.

Key Terms
فاسد (fasid): A corrupt contract that is legally defective but may produce some effects, such as obligation of ujr al-mithl.
ضمين (damin): Liable or guarantor; here, the trustee is not damin for the shortfall in rent.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a trustee leases waqf property for a rent that is grossly below market (ujr al-mithl), who must pay the difference?
A) The trustee from his own pocket
B) The lessee must pay ujr al-mithl
C) The judge must compensate the waqf
D) No one; the waqf suffers the loss

Key Takeaway
The trustee is not personally liable for leasing below market; the lessee must pay the equivalent rent.

Judge's Authority to Lease Waqf with Existing Trustee
Resolves whether the judge can lease waqf property when a trustee is already appointed

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on when a judge may lease waqf property and apply the principle that specific authority (wilayah khassah) overrides general authority (wilayah 'ammah).

Core Idea
The trustee's specific authority overrides the judge's general authority; the judge only steps in when the trustee fails.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Raiq delves into a subtle intra-Hanafi debate: does the judge have the authority to lease waqf property when a trustee is already appointed? The discussion hinges on the distinction between general and specific authority, a key concept in Hanafi legal theory. By the end, you will understand the settled view and its practical implications for waqf administration.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue
What is the legal question being debated? The passage asks whether the judge can lease waqf property when a specific trustee (mutawalli) exists.
The debate arises from the tension between the judge's general oversight of endowments and the trustee's designated role.
2
State the initial position
The author initially responds that the judge does have such authority, citing al-Isturushani's general statement.
The author's quick answer seems absolute, but it is challenged.
3
Identify the opposing position and its proof
Al-Ramli critiques the initial answer and argues that the judge does not have authority when a trustee exists. He invokes the rule 'specific authority is stronger than general authority' and draws an analogy to the prohibition on judges disposing of orphan property when a guardian exists.
This opposing view relies on a fundamental principle of Hanafi hierarchy of authorities.
4
Understand the governing principle
The principle 'al-wilayah al-khassah aqwa min al-wilayah al-'ammah' – specific authority is stronger than general authority – is the key. The trustee holds specific authority over the waqf, so the judge's general authority is secondary and only activated when the specific authority is absent or dysfunctional.
This principle is cited from al-Ashbah wa al-Naza'ir and is widely applied in Hanafi fiqh.
5
Determine the settled position
The relied-upon (mufta bihi) position is that the judge may lease the waqf only if the trustee refuses to do so or is incapable. Otherwise, the trustee retains exclusive authority.
The passage shows that the apparent absoluteness of al-Isturushani's words is interpreted as applying only when the trustee fails.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قلت : هل للقاضي ولاية الإيجار مع وجود المتولي؟ قلت : نعم على ما قدمناه أجرة الأرض في نفس الأمر يفسخها في خلال المدة أيضاً ولا يجوز إبقاؤها بحال . اهـ ملخصاً. قوله : (فإن قلت الخ، سئل هل للقاضي أن يؤجر مع بقاء الناظر ؟ فأجاب : نص الاستروشني على أن إجارة الموقوف عليه لا تجوز وإنما يملك الإجارة المتولي أو القاضي وهذا بإطلاقه يقتضي للقاضي ذلك ولو كان للوقف متول لكن نصهم على أن القاضي محجور من التصرف في مال اليتيم وصي الميت وعند من نصبه القاضي عن الميت يقتضي بالقياس عليه أن القاضي إنما يؤجر إذا لم يكن للوقف متول أو كان له متول لكن امتنع من الإيجار ويكون هذا محمل كلام الآستروشني والله أعلم. فتاوى حانوتي قوله : قلت نعم قال الرملي : الذي قدمه لا يفيد القطع بالحكم بل التردد فيه. وأقول : الظاهر لا ويدل عليه قولهم الولاية الخاصة أقوى من الولاية العامة، فيحمل ما هنا على ما إذا أبى المتولي إجارتها فتأمل. وقد قال في الأشباه بعد ما فرع على القاعدة المذكورة: وعلى هذا لا يملك القاضي التصرف في الوقف مع وجود ناظر ولو من قبله والإجارة تصرف في الوقف بخلاف تقرير الوظائف لغير المشروط له ذلك فإنه تصرف في الموقوف عليهم تأمل. وفي أوقاف هلال : أرأيت القاضي إذا أجر الدار الوقف قال : الإجارة جائزة . قلت : وكذلك لو أجرها وكيل القاضي بأمره؟ قال : نعم. وظاهره إطلاق الجواز مع وجود المتولي ووجهه ظاهر . اهـ كلام الرملي ملخصاً
If you say: Does the judge have the authority to lease when a trustee exists? I say: Yes, based on what we have presented, the land's rent in itself, he rescinds it during the term as well and it is not permissible to leave it as it is. Summary. His statement: (If you say...), it was asked: Can the judge lease while the overseer remains? He answered: The text of al-Isturushani states that leasing by the beneficiary of the waqf is not permissible; only the trustee or judge may lease, and this in its absoluteness necessitates that the judge can do so even if there is a trustee. But their texts state that the judge is prohibited from disposing of the orphan's property if there is a guardian appointed by the deceased, and by analogy, the judge only leases if there is no trustee or if the trustee refuses. This is the interpretation of al-Isturushani's words, and Allah knows best. From Fatawa Hanuti. His statement: I said yes. Al-Ramli said: What he presented does not give certainty of the ruling, but rather hesitation. And I say: The apparent is no, and their statement 'specific authority is stronger than general authority' supports it. So what is here should be interpreted as applying when the trustee refuses to lease. Consider. And it was said in al-Ashbah after branching on the mentioned rule: On this, the judge does not have the right to dispose of the waqf when a trustee exists, even if appointed by him, and leasing is a disposition of the waqf, unlike the appointment of officials for other than those stipulated, for that is a disposition on the beneficiaries. Consider. And in Awqaf Hilal: 'Do you see if the judge leases the waqf house?' He said: 'The lease is permissible.' I said: 'Likewise, if the judge's agent leases by his order?' He said: 'Yes.' Its apparent is the absolute permissibility even when a trustee exists, and its reasoning is evident. End of al-Ramli's speech, summarized.
Source pages p. 400

Commentary
This passage from Al-Bahr al-Raiq addresses a point of intra-Hanafi debate: whether the judge (qadi) has the authority to lease waqf property when a specific trustee (mutawalli) is already appointed. The dialogue format presents two views. The author first answers succinctly that the judge does have that authority, and may even rescind existing leases if they are disadvantageous. However, al-Ramli critiques this, arguing that the ruling is not definitive; he prefers the view that the judge cannot lease when a trustee exists, citing the principle 'specific authority (wilayah khassah) is stronger than general authority (wilayah 'ammah).' The judge's general authority over all endowments is secondary to the trustee's specific authority. The passage then cites al-Ashbah wa al-Naza'ir, which states that the judge cannot dispose of the waqf in the presence of a trustee, especially since leasing is a disposition of the waqf itself. Yet, there is a counter-opinion from Awqaf Hilal that permits the judge to lease even with a trustee. The resolution is contextual: if the trustee refuses or neglects his duty, the judge may step in. The relied-upon position in the Hanafi school is that the judge's authority is supplementary, not overriding; he may only lease if the trustee is absent, incapable, or refusing. The passage demonstrates the internal reasoning of the school: weighing general vs. specific authority and the practical need to protect the waqf.

Key Terms
الولاية الخاصة (al-wilayah al-khassah): The specific authority of the appointed trustee (mutawalli) over the waqf, which overrides the general authority of the judge.
الولاية العامة (al-wilayah al-'ammah): The general authority of the judge over all waqfs and minors, which acts only when no specific trustee exists or fails.
المتولي (al-mutawalli): The trustee or administrator of the waqf, appointed by the waqif or by the judge in absence of appointment.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position, when can a judge lease waqf property if a trustee is present?
A) At any time, since the judge has general authority
B) Only if the trustee refuses to lease or is incapable
C) Never, even if the trustee is negligent
D) Only with the permission of the waqif's descendants

Key Takeaway
The judge's general authority over waqfs is secondary: the trustee's specific authority takes precedence, and the judge may only lease when the trustee fails to act.

Rulings on Waqif Leasing Below Market and Conditions
Rulings on waqf leasing and conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling when a waqif leases waqf property below market rate and identify when the waqif's conditions are binding on the mutawalli.

Core Idea
Leasing waqf below market is invalid; the waqif's conditions are binding if they do not contravene Shari'ah.

The waqf is a charitable endowment meant to benefit the public. Its preservation and optimal use are paramount. This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq addresses two key scenarios: when the waqif himself leases the waqf property at a rent significantly below the market rate, and when the waqif sets conditions in the waqf deed that restrict how the mutawalli may manage the property. Understanding these rules ensures the waqf's purpose is fulfilled and that its administrator acts within proper bounds.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling for below-market leasing
The passage states that if the waqif leases the waqf for a rent that is significantly below the market rate (i.e., what people would normally disagree about), the lease is not permissible (ghayr ja'iz) and the judge (qadi) must annul it. This is the base ruling.
The rent is compared to 'ujrat al-mithl (market rent). A significant deviation triggers invalidity.
2
Distinguish based on the waqif's trustworthiness
Although the lease is invalid, the judge's response varies: if the waqif is trustworthy and the below-market leasing was a mistake or negligence, the judge leaves the waqf in his hands but orders him to lease at a proper rate. If the waqif is not trustworthy, the judge removes him and appoints another trustee.
The waqif's character affects the remedy but not the initial invalidity.
3
Apply the same ruling to the mutawalli
The text explicitly extends this ruling to the mutawalli (trustee), stating that if the waqif is subject to these restrictions, the mutawalli is even more so. Thus, the mutawalli must not lease below market without valid reason.
This is a fortiori (awlā) reasoning.
4
Analyze the binding nature of the waqif's conditions
The passage from al-Is'af lists examples of conditions the waqif may set, such as prohibiting lease, sharecropping, or working on trees, or limiting the lease term. It declares such conditions valid (mu'tabir) and that violating them is not permitted.
This reflects the Hanafi principle that the waqif's stipulations are binding as long as they do not contradict Shari'ah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وذكر الخصاف أن الواقف أيضاً إذا أجر بالأقل مما لا يتغابن الناس في مثله فإنها غير جائزة ويبطلها القاضي فإن كان الواقف مأموناً وفعل ذلك على طريق السهو والغفلة أقره القاضي في يده وأمره بإجارتها بالأصلح، وإن كان غير مأمون أخرجها من يده وجعلها في يد من يثق بدينه وكذا إذا أجرها الواقف سنين كثيرة ممن يخاف أن تتلف في يده قال : يبطل القاضي الإجارة ويخرجها من يد المستأجر .اهـ. فإذا كان هذا في الواقف فالمتولي أولى . وفي الإسعاف : لو شرط الواقف أن لا يؤجر المتولي الوقف ولا شيئاً منه أو أن لا يدفعه مزارعة أو على أن لا يعمل على ما فيه من الأشجار أو شرط أن لا يؤجر إلا ثلاث سنين ثم لا يعقد عليه إلا بعد انقضاء العقد الأول كان شرطه معتبراً ولا تجوز مخالفته اهـ.
And al-Khassaf mentioned that if the waqif himself leases [the waqf] for less than what people would normally disagree [i.e., significantly below market], then it is not permissible and the judge annuls it. If the waqif is trustworthy and did that out of oversight and negligence, the judge confirms it in his hand and orders him to lease it at the appropriate rate. If he is not trustworthy, the judge removes it from his hand and places it in the hand of someone whose religion he trusts. Similarly, if the waqif leases it for many years to someone who is feared to cause its destruction, he said: The judge annuls the lease and removes it from the tenant's hand. So if this is the case for the waqif, then the mutawalli is even more [deserving of this ruling]. And in al-Is'af: If the waqif stipulates that the mutawalli should not lease the waqf or any part of it, or that he should not give it as a sharecropping contract, or that he should not work on its trees, or stipulates that he should not lease except for three years and then not contract again except after the expiry of the first contract, then his condition is valid and violating it is not permitted.
Source pages p. 400

Commentary
This passage begins by stating a ruling from al-Khassaf regarding a waqif who leases his waqf property for a rent significantly below the market rate. Such an act is invalid (ghayr ja'iz) and the qadi (judge) is to annul the lease. However, if the waqif is a trustworthy person and did this out of carelessness, the qadi leaves the waqf in his hands but orders him to lease it at the proper rate. If he is not trustworthy, the qadi removes the waqf from his control and appoints someone else. Similarly, if the waqif leases the waqf for many years to a person who is likely to cause the waqf property to be destroyed, the qadi annuls the lease. The text then states that if this ruling applies to the waqif himself, it applies even more to the mutawalli (trustee), as the mutawalli is subject to the same restrictions. Next, the passage quotes al-Is'af regarding conditions set by the waqif. If the waqif stipulates in his waqf deed that the mutawalli cannot lease the waqf, or cannot give it as a sharecropping contract, or cannot work on its trees, or can only lease for three years and then must wait until the contract ends before renewing, such conditions are valid (mu'tabir) and must be followed. Violating these conditions is not permitted. This emphasizes the principle that the waqif's stipulations are binding as long as they do not contradict the Shari'ah.

Key Terms
الواقف (al-wāqif): The person who creates the waqf (endowment).
المتولي (al-mutawallī): The trustee or manager of the waqf property.
إجارة (ijārah): Leasing or renting of property.
شرط (sharṭ): A condition stipulated by the waqif in the waqf deed.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage from al-Is'af, if a waqif stipulates that the mutawalli shall not lease the waqf for more than three years, what is the ruling?
A) The condition is invalid because it restricts the mutawalli.
B) The condition is valid and must be followed.
C) The condition is valid but can be ignored if a better offer arises.
D) The condition is invalid because lease terms are always at the mutawalli's discretion.

Key Takeaway
Leasing waqf below market is invalid and the judge annuls it; the waqif's binding conditions restrict the mutawalli and must be obeyed.

When Can a Qadi Lease Waqf? The Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on qadi's leasing authority vs mutawalli.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the mufta bihi Hanafi position on the qadi's authority to lease waqf when a mutawalli is present and identify the thamarah al-khilaf.

Core Idea
The mutawalli's specific authority prevents the qadi from leasing unless the mutawalli refuses.

Who controls waqf leasing when both a qadi and a mutawalli exist? This passage from al-Bahr al-Rāʾiq presents an intra-Hanafi dispute that turns on the hierarchy of authorities: does the qadi's general jurisdiction (wilāyah ʿāmmah) override the mutawalli's specific charge (wilāyah khāṣṣah)? By tracing the arguments and the resolution, you will understand how later Hanafi masters reconciled conflicting texts and why the restricted view became the operative position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Core Question
Recognize that the dispute asks whether the qadi can lease waqf property when a specific supervisor (mutawalli/nāẓir) is already appointed and willing.
The question is framed in the text as 'هل للقاضي ولاية الإيجار مع وجود المتولي؟'.
2
Isolate the Two Positions
Extract the absolute view (al-Ustarushani's text and Awqāf Hilāl) allowing qadi's lease regardless of mutawalli, and the restricted view (al-Ramlī, al-Ashbāh) limiting it to cases of mutawalli's absence or refusal.
3
Examine the Key Principle and Analogy
The restricted view relies on the maxim 'al-wilāyah al-khāṣṣah aqwā min al-wilāyah al-ʿāmmah' and an analogy (qiyās) to the qadi's prohibition from dealing with an orphan's property when a guardian exists.
The qiyās is jaliyy (clear), as stated in the text: 'يقتضي بالقياس عليه أن القاضي إنما يؤجر إذا لم يكن للوقف متول'.
4
Apply the Thamarah al-Khilaf
Determine the practical consequence: if a willing mutawalli exists, the qadi's lease is bāṭil; if the mutawalli refuses, the qadi's lease is ṣaḥīḥ.
This difference directly affects the validity of real contracts involving waqf properties.
5
Confirm the Mufta Bihi
Recognize that al-Ramlī and the author of al-Ashbāh favored the restricted view, making it the relied-upon position in the school.
The text signals tarjīh by stating 'وعلى هذا لا يملك القاضي التصرف في الوقف مع وجود ناظر'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قلت : هل للقاضي ولاية الإيجار مع وجود المتولي؟ قلت : نعم على ما قدمناه أجرة الأرض في نفس الأمر يفسخها في خلال المدة أيضاً ولا يجوز إبقاؤها بحال . اهـ ملخصاً. قوله : (فإن قلت الخ، سئل هل للقاضي أن يؤجر مع بقاء الناظر ؟ فأجاب : نص الاستروشني على أن إجارة الموقوف عليه لا تجوز وإنما يملك الإجارة المتولي أو القاضي وهذا بإطلاقه يقتضي للقاضي ذلك ولو كان للوقف متول لكن نصهم على أن القاضي محجور من التصرف في مال اليتيم وصي الميت وعند من نصبه القاضي عن الميت يقتضي بالقياس عليه أن القاضي إنما يؤجر إذا لم يكن للوقف متول أو كان له متول لكن امتنع من الإيجار ويكون هذا محمل كلام الآستروشني والله أعلم. فتاوى حانوتي قوله : قلت نعم قال الرملي : الذي قدمه لا يفيد القطع بالحكم بل التردد فيه. وأقول : الظاهر لا ويدل عليه قولهم الولاية الخاصة أقوى من الولاية العامة، فيحمل ما هنا على ما إذا أبى المتولي إجارتها فتأمل. وقد قال في الأشباه بعد ما فرع على القاعدة المذكورة: وعلى هذا لا يملك القاضي التصرف في الوقف مع وجود ناظر ولو من قبله والإجارة تصرف في الوقف بخلاف تقرير الوظائف لغير المشروط له ذلك فإنه تصرف في الموقوف عليهم تأمل. وفي أوقاف هلال : أرأيت القاضي إذا أجر الدار الوقف قال : الإجارة جائزة . قلت : وكذلك لو أجرها وكيل القاضي بأمره؟ قال : نعم. وظاهره إطلاق الجواز مع وجود المتولي ووجهه ظاهر . اهـ كلام الرملي
If you ask: Does the qadi have the authority to lease when a mutawalli is present? I say: Yes, based on what we presented regarding the rent of land in reality; he can annul it even during the term and it is not permissible to keep it at all. [Summary] His statement: (If you ask...) It was asked: Can the qadi lease while a supervisor exists? He answered: The text of al-Ustarushani states that the leasing by the beneficiary of the waqf is not permissible; only the mutawalli or the qadi possesses the right to lease. This, in its absolute sense, implies that the qadi has that right even if the waqf has a mutawalli. However, their texts state that the qadi is prevented from dealing with the property of an orphan and the executor of the deceased; by analogy, the qadi only leases if there is no mutawalli for the waqf, or if there is a mutawalli but he refuses to lease. This is the interpretation of al-Ustarushani's words. And Allah knows best. (From Fatawa Hanuti) His statement: 'I say: Yes' – al-Ramli said: What he presented does not give certainty of the ruling but rather hesitation. I say: The apparent view is no, as supported by their statement: A specific authority (wilayah khassah) is stronger than a general authority (wilayah 'ammah). Therefore, what is [stated] here is to be interpreted as [referring to] when the mutawalli refuses to lease – so ponder. And in al-Ashbah, after branching on the mentioned principle: On this basis, the qadi does not have authority over waqf disposal when a supervisor exists, even if appointed by him, and leasing is a disposal of the waqf, unlike the appointment of officials for those not entitled, for that is a disposal concerning the beneficiaries – ponder. And in Awqaf Hilal: Did you see if the qadi leased the waqf property? He said: The lease is valid. I said: Similarly, if the qadi's agent leased it by his order? He said: Yes. The apparent meaning is the permissibility absolutely even with a mutawalli present, and its reasoning is clear. End of al-Ramli's words.
Source pages p. 400

Commentary
This passage deals with a disputed issue: Does the qadi have the authority to lease waqf property when a mutawalli is present? The passage presents multiple opinions. First, a question is posed and answered affirmatively based on an earlier statement about the qadi's authority to annul leases. The text then cites al-Ustarushani, who states that only the mutawalli or the qadi may lease, seemingly giving the qadi absolute authority. However, the author notes that by analogy to the qadi's restricted role in dealing with an orphan's property, the qadi should only lease when no mutawalli exists or when the mutawalli refuses. Al-Ramli comments that the initial answer is not conclusive; he leans toward the view that the qadi does not have authority because a specific authority (wilayah khassah of the mutawalli) outweighs a general authority (wilayah 'ammah of the qadi). This is reinforced by a quote from al-Ashbah. Finally, a report from Awqaf Hilal suggests the qadi's lease is valid in all cases, which seems to contradict. The passage thus presents an intra-madhhab dispute. The relied-upon position is that the qadi's authority is restricted to cases where the mutawalli refuses or is absent.

Key Terms
ولاية خاصة (wilāyah khāṣṣah): Specific authority, e.g., that of a mutawalli over the waqf he manages.
ولاية عامة (wilāyah ʿāmmah): General authority, e.g., that of a qadi over all affairs within his jurisdiction.
الناظر (al-nāẓir): Another term for mutawalli, the supervisor of the waqf.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon view in the Hanafi school, when can a qadi lease waqf property if a mutawalli is present?
A) At any time, because the qadi has general authority.
B) Only if the mutawalli is absent or refuses to lease.
C) Only with the mutawalli's permission.
D) Never, because the mutawalli has exclusive authority.

Key Takeaway
The qadi cannot lease waqf property against a willing mutawalli because the mutawalli's specific authority (wilāyah khāṣṣah) overrides the qadi's general authority.

Mutawalli’s Leasing for Goods and Liability for Negligence
Compiles rulings on leasing, lending, and liability of the trustee.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi dispute on leasing waqf for goods and distinguish when the trustee is liable for negligence (ayn vs dhimma).

Core Idea
A trustee is liable for physical assets but not for debts, and leasing for goods is disputed within the school.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq collects several important rulings for the mutawalli of a waqf. It begins with an intra-Hanafi dispute about whether the trustee can lease waqf property in exchange for goods (barter) rather than cash. It then addresses the trustee's liability when following a qadi's order, lending waqf funds, and the crucial distinction between liability for a physical asset (ayn) versus a debt (dhimma). These distinctions are essential for any trustee to avoid personal liability.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dispute on leasing for goods
Locate the sentence 'وتجوز إجارة القيم الوقف بعرض عند أبي حنيفة خلافاً لهما' – this is the core dispute. Note Abu Hanifa's permissibility and the two disciples' disapproval.
The exception for father and executor (الأب والوصي) shows consensus when acting for an orphan.
2
Analyze the ruling on lending waqf funds
Read the paragraphs about lending. The general rule is prohibition: the trustee cannot lend waqf funds. If he does, he and the borrower are liable. However, an exception exists if lending is safer and the purpose is to retrieve later.
The qadi's order does not absolve liability if the action is inherently prohibited.
3
Distinguish liability for 'ayn vs dhimma
The passage states: 'إن كان في عين ضمنها وإن كان فيما في الذمة لا يضمن'. The trustee is liable for physical assets (e.g., the wood of a collapsed masjid) but not for debts (e.g., the unpaid price of paint). Understand the key terms: 'ayn = tangible asset, dhimma = abstract obligation.
This distinction is crucial for determining whether the trustee must compensate.
4
Examine the case of contractors (Bazzaziyya)
If the trustee refrains from demanding payment from contractors, he does not sin. If a contractor flees after a debt is due, the trustee is not liable because the debt is in the dhimma.
This reinforces the ayn/dhimma distinction.
5
Consider the overall principle
Synthesize: The trustee must protect the corpus (ayn) but has leniency in matters of debt (dhimma). The intra-Hanafi dispute on barter leasing shows that not all matters are settled; later practice may favor caution.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي الظهيرية وتجوز إجارة القيم الوقف بعرض عند أبي حنيفة خلافاً لهما، والأب والوصي ! إذا أجر داراً لليتيم بعرض جاز بلا خلاف. وفي القنية : ولا يجوز للقيم شراء شيء من مال المسجد لنفسه ولا البيع له وإن كان فيه منفعة ظاهرة للمسجد اهـ. فإن قلت : إذا أمر القاضي بشيء ففعله ثم تبين أنه ليس بشرعي أو فيه ضرر على الوقف هل يكون القيم ضامناً؟ قلت : قال في القنية طالب القيم أهل المحلة أن يقرض من مال المسجد للإمام فأبى فأمره القاضي به فأقرضه ثم مات الإمام مفلساً لا يضمن القيم أن القيم ليس له إقراض مال المسجد قال في جامع الفصولين: ليس للمتولي إيداع مال الوقف والمسجد إلا ممن في عياله ولا إقراضه فلو أقرضه ضمن وكذا المستقرض، وذكر أن القيم لو أقرض مال المسجد ليأخذه عند الحاجة وهو أحرز من إمساكه فلا بأس به. وفي المتولي إقراض ما فضل من غلة الوقف لو أحرز .اهـ. فإن قلت : إذا قصر المتولي في شيء من مصالح الوقف هل يضمن؟ قلت : إن كان في عين ضمنها وإن كان فيما في الذمة لا يضمن. قال في القنية : انهدم المسجد فلم يحفظه القيم حتى ضاعت خشبة يضمن اشترى القيم من الدهان دهناً ودع الثمن ثم أفلس الدهان بعد لم يضمن اهـ. وفي البزازية : امتنع المتولي عن تقاضي ما على المتقبلين لا يأثم، فإن هرب بعض المتقبلين بعدما اجتمع عليه مال كثير بحق القبالة لا يضمن المتولي اهـ. وفي القنية : أجر القيم ثم عزل ونصب قيم آخر قلت: وجدت في التجنيس ما يؤخذ منه جواب المسألة ونصه أرض وقف بدرعم وهي ناحية من نواحي سمرقند ولها متول من جهة قاضي سمرقند فاستأجرها رجل من حاكم بدراهم معلومة فزرعها فلما حصلت الغلة طلب المتولي الحصة من الغلة كما جرى العرف في المزارعة بدرعم فقال الرجل على الأجرة كان للمتولي أن يأخذ الحصة لأنه لا ولاية للحاكم لأن تولية القاضي لهذا المتولي إن كان قبل تقليد الحاكم لم يدخل ذلك في تقليده وإن كان بعد تقليده خرج الحاكم عن ولاية تلك الأرض فلم تصح إجارته، فإذا زرعها وقد جرى العرف بالمزارعة على النصف أو على الثلث صار كأن المتولي دفعها إليه مزارعة على ذلك .اهـ. ونحوه في التتارخانية. وقد ذكرها في الإسعاف أيضاً في فصل إجارة الوقف بأوضح بأن الحاكم من جهة قاضي البلدة، ولا يخفى من هذه العبارة وصرح . أن العدول عن التعليل بأن القاضي أو مأموره ليس له ولاية الإيجار مع حضور المتولي إلى التعليل بما أنه لم يدخل في تعليله أو خارج عنه يفيد ملك القاضي لذلك تأمل. قوله : (وفي القنية أجر القيم ثم عزل الخ) قال الرملي : قد أفتى الشارح بأن أخذها للمعزول وهي في فتاواه ولم ينقل خلافه وقد علم بما ذكر أنه إفتاء بخلاف الأصح قوله : (للقيم صرف شيء من مال الوقف إلى كتبة ذكره من البحر الرائق ٥/ ٢٦٥
And in al-Zahiriya: Leasing the waqf by the trustee for [exchange of] goods is permissible according to Abu Hanifa, contrary to the two [disciples]. The father and executor, if they lease a house for an orphan in exchange for goods, are permitted without disagreement. In al-Qunyah: The trustee is not permitted to buy anything from the masjid's wealth for himself, nor to sell to himself, even if there is an apparent benefit to the masjid. If you ask: If the qadi ordered something and he did it, then it became clear that it was not in accordance with Shari'ah or causes harm to the waqf, is the trustee liable? I say: It is stated in al-Qunyah: The trustee asked the people of the neighborhood to lend from the masjid's wealth to the imam, but they refused, so the qadi ordered him to do so, and he lent, then the imam died bankrupt; the trustee is not liable. The trustee does not have the right to lend the masjid's wealth. In Jami' al-Fusulayn: The trustee is not allowed to deposit the waqf or masjid's wealth except with someone in his household, nor to lend it. If he lends it, he is liable, and similarly the borrower. It is mentioned that if the trustee lends the masjid's wealth to take it back when needed and it is safer than keeping it, there is no harm. And the trustee lending the surplus of waqf produce if it is safe. If you ask: If the trustee is negligent in some matters of the waqf's interests, is he liable? I say: If it concerns a physical asset [ayn], he is liable; if it concerns a debt [dhimma], he is not liable. It is stated in al-Qunyah: The masjid collapsed and the trustee did not preserve it until the wood was lost; he is liable. The trustee bought paint from the painter and left the price, then the painter became insolvent; he is not liable. In al-Bazzaziyya: If the trustee refrains from demanding what is due from the contractors, he does not sin. If some contractors flee after a large sum of money is due from them, the trustee is not liable. In al-Qunyah: The trustee leased [the waqf], then was removed, and another trustee was appointed. I say: I found in al-Tajnis an answer to the question: The text: A waqf land in Bad'am, a district of Samarqand, has a trustee appointed by the qadi of Samarqand. A man leased it from the governor for a known amount of dirhams and cultivated it. When the produce came, the trustee demanded a share of the produce as is custom in sharecropping in Bad'am. The man said: [I paid] rent. The trustee is entitled to take a share because the governor had no authority. Because the appointment of this trustee by the qadi, if it was before the governor's appointment [taqlid], then that [authority] did not enter into his appointment; if it was after, then the governor had no authority over that land, so his lease is invalid. Since he cultivated it and the custom of sharecropping was on half or third, it becomes as if the trustee gave it to him as sharecropping on that. Similarly in al-Tatar Khaniyya. And it is mentioned in al-Is'af also in the chapter on waqf leasing more clearly: that the governor is from the side of the qadi of the town. It is not hidden from this statement that the shift from the reasoning that the qadi or his agent has no authority to lease when the mutawalli is present, to the reasoning that it did not enter into his appointment or is outside it, gives the qadi ownership of that? Ponder. His statement: (In al-Qunyah, the trustee leased then was removed...) al-Ramli said: The commentator gave a fatwa that taking it [the lease] for the removed trustee is [valid], and it is in his fatawa, and no disagreement was transmitted. It is known from what was mentioned that it is a fatwa contrary to the correct [view]. His statement: (The trustee may spend something from the waqf's wealth on... mentioned from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq 5/265
Source pages p. 401

Commentary
This passage compiles multiple rulings from various works. It begins with an intra-Hanafi dispute: According to Abu Hanifa, it is permissible for the trustee of a waqf to lease the waqf in exchange for goods (e.g., barter), but Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani disagree. However, for an orphan's property, all agree the guardian can do so. Next, it states that the trustee cannot buy from waqf funds for himself. The passage then discusses liability: if the qadi orders something that turns out harmful, the trustee is not liable if he was not at fault. The ruling on lending waqf money is that it is generally not allowed; the trustee is liable if he lends without authorization, but if it is for safekeeping (like taking back when needed and safer), there is no harm. On negligence: if the trustee fails to preserve a physical asset (like the wood of a collapsed masjid), he is liable; but for debts (like leaving the price of paint), he is not. If the trustee refrains from demanding payments from contractors, he does not sin and is not liable if they flee. A case from al-Tajnis: A waqf land was leased by a governor, but the trustee challenges the lease because the governor had no authority; the ruling treats it as a sharecropping arrangement based on custom. Finally, a note that a fatwa by the commentator (Ibn Nujaym) allows the removed trustee to collect the lease, but al-Ramli says this is contrary to the correct view.

Key Terms
القيم (al-qayyim): The trustee or caretaker of a waqf or masjid; synonym of mutawalli.
العرض (al-ʿarḍ): Goods or commodities as opposed to currency; used in barter leasing.
الضمان (al-ḍamān): Liability or guarantee for loss or damage.
عين (ʿayn): Physical asset or property.
ذمة (dhimma): Debt or obligation in the abstract; a liability in the conscience.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if the trustee lends waqf money without authorization, what is the ruling?
A) He is not liable because he acted in good faith.
B) He is liable, and the borrower is also liable.
C) He is liable only if the borrower defaults.
D) He is not liable if the qadi ordered it.

Key Takeaway
The trustee's liability hinges on whether the loss concerns a physical asset (ayn) or a debt (dhimma); lending waqf funds is generally prohibited and creates dual liability.

Special Cases: Governor’s Lease and Removed Trustee’s Authority
Specific case studies on authority and custom in waqf leasing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why a governor’s lease of waqf land may be invalid and state the ruling on who collects rent after a trustee is removed.

Core Idea
A governor may not lease waqf land if the mutawalli is present; custom may convert an invalid lease into sharecropping.

This lesson examines a detailed case from al-Tajnis involving a waqf land leased by a governor rather than the appointed mutawalli. The passage explores the jurisdictional issue: did the governor have authority? The resolution uses local custom to treat the invalid lease as a sharecropping arrangement. Additionally, a later note discusses a fatwa by the commentator (Ibn Nujaym) allowing a removed trustee to collect rent, which al-Ramli criticizes. These cases illustrate how practical rulings can depend on authority, custom, and scholarly disagreement.

Guided Path
1
Identify the case from al-Tajnis
Read the sentence beginning 'أرض وقف بدرعم...' – a man leased waqf land from the governor, cultivated it, and the trustee demanded a share of the crop per local custom. The lessee claimed he paid rent.
The key question: did the governor have authority to lease?
2
Analyze the authority of the governor
The passage explains: if the qadi appointed the trustee before the governor's appointment, the governor had no authority over that land; if after, the governor lost authority because the trustee was already appointed. In either case, the governor's lease is invalid.
This shows the importance of proper delegation in waqf management.
3
Understand the conversion to sharecropping
Since the lease was invalid but the tenant cultivated with consent, and local custom was sharecropping, the law treats it as a muzzara'ah (sharecropping) contract. The trustee can demand the customary share (half or third).
Custom ('urf) plays a role in shaping the legal outcome.
4
Examine the fatwa on the removed trustee
The passage says: 'أجر القيم ثم عزل ونصب قيم آخر...' then al-Ramli notes that the commentator (Ibn Nujaym) gave a fatwa that the removed trustee may collect the rent. But al-Ramli says this is contrary to the correct view (al-asahh).
This is another intra-Hanafi dispute: who is entitled to the rent after removal?
5
Consider the tarjih
The correct view (according to al-Ramli) is that the removed trustee no longer has authority; the new trustee collects the rent. However, the commentator's fatwa may have been based on some custom or necessity.
No explicit resolution is given in the text; it highlights ongoing disagreement.

The father and executor, if they lease a house for an orphan in exchange for goods, are permitted without disagreement. In al-Qunyah: The trustee is not permitted to buy anything from the masjid's wealth for himself, nor to sell to himself, even if there is an apparent benefit to the masjid. If you ask: If the qadi ordered something and he did it, then it became clear that it was not in accordance with Shari'ah or causes harm to the waqf, is the trustee liable? I say: It is stated in al-Qunyah: The trustee asked the people of the neighborhood to lend from the masjid's wealth to the imam, but they refused, so the qadi ordered him to do so, and he lent, then the imam died bankrupt; the trustee is not liable. The trustee does not have the right to lend the masjid's wealth. In Jami' al-Fusulayn: The trustee is not allowed to deposit the waqf or masjid's wealth except with someone in his household, nor to lend it. If he lends it, he is liable, and similarly the borrower. It is mentioned that if the trustee lends the masjid's wealth to take it back when needed and it is safer than keeping it, there is no harm. A man leased it from the governor for a known amount of dirhams and cultivated it. When the produce came, the trustee demanded a share of the produce as is custom in sharecropping in Bad'am. The man said: [I paid] rent. The trustee is entitled to take a share because the governor had no authority. Because the appointment of this trustee by the qadi, if it was before the governor's appointment [taqlid], then that [authority] did not enter into his appointment; if it was after, then the governor had no authority over that land, so his lease is invalid. Since he cultivated it and the custom of sharecropping was on half or third, it becomes as if the trustee gave it to him as sharecropping on that. Similarly in al-Tatar Khaniyya. And it is mentioned in al-Is'af also in the chapter on waqf leasing more clearly: that the governor is from the side of the qadi of the town. It is not hidden from this statement that the shift from the reasoning that the qadi or his agent has no authority to lease when the mutawalli is present, to the reasoning that it did not enter into his appointment or is outside it, gives the qadi ownership of that? Ponder. It is known from what was mentioned that it is a fatwa contrary to the correct [view]. His statement: (The trustee may spend something from the waqf's wealth on... mentioned from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq 5/265
Source pages p. 401

Commentary
This passage compiles multiple rulings from various works. It begins with an intra-Hanafi dispute: According to Abu Hanifa, it is permissible for the trustee of a waqf to lease the waqf in exchange for goods (e.g., barter), but Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani disagree. However, for an orphan's property, all agree the guardian can do so. Next, it states that the trustee cannot buy from waqf funds for himself. The passage then discusses liability: if the qadi orders something that turns out harmful, the trustee is not liable if he was not at fault. The ruling on lending waqf money is that it is generally not allowed; the trustee is liable if he lends without authorization, but if it is for safekeeping (like taking back when needed and safer), there is no harm. On negligence: if the trustee fails to preserve a physical asset (like the wood of a collapsed masjid), he is liable; but for debts (like leaving the price of paint), he is not. If the trustee refrains from demanding payments from contractors, he does not sin and is not liable if they flee. A case from al-Tajnis: A waqf land was leased by a governor, but the trustee challenges the lease because the governor had no authority; the ruling treats it as a sharecropping arrangement based on custom. Finally, a note that a fatwa by the commentator (Ibn Nujaym) allows the removed trustee to collect the lease, but al-Ramli says this is contrary to the correct view.

Key Terms
القيم (al-qayyim): The trustee or caretaker of a waqf; synonym of mutawalli.
الحاكم (al-ḥākim): Governor or local ruler; may have authority over certain lands.
المزارعة (al-muzāraʿa): Sharecropping; a contract where landowner and cultivator share the produce.
العرف (al-ʿurf): Custom; used to determine shares in sharecropping.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what happens when a governor leases waqf land without authority but the tenant cultivates and local custom is sharecropping?
A) The lease is void and the tenant owes nothing.
B) The lease is valid because the tenant acted in good faith.
C) The lease is invalid but the tenant owes the customary share as in sharecropping.
D) The trustee must ratify the lease to make it valid.

Key Takeaway
A lease by an unauthorized party is invalid, but if the tenant cultivated with consent, local custom may transform it into a sharecropping arrangement.

Leasing Waqf for Goods and Corrupt Leases
Expansion on the matn statement 'أجرها الحاكم' regarding leasing waqf.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to differentiate Abu Hanifa's and the Sahibayn's views on leasing waqf for merchandise, and explain the ruling on rent in a corrupt lease when no benefit is taken.

Core Idea
A corrupt waqf lease yields no rent if unused; leasing for goods is valid per Abu Hanifa but disputed.

The waqf administrator must navigate specific rules when leasing property. This lesson focuses on two related areas: the effect of a corrupt lease (ijarah fasidah) and the permissibility of accepting payment in goods (al-'ard) instead of cash. These rulings have direct implications for the administrator's liability and the waqf's income.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two rulings
Locate the ruling on corrupt lease and on leasing for goods in the passage.
The corrupt lease rule is from al-Khassaf; the goods dispute is from al-Zahiriyyah.
2
Analyze the corrupt lease rule
Understand why no rent is due if the lessee did not benefit. In waqf, mere ability to use does not obligate rent.
This is specific to waqf; differs from regular leases.
3
Examine the intra-Hanafi dispute
Abu Hanifa permits leasing for goods; Abu Yusuf and Muhammad prohibit it. Note the exception for father/guardian.
The dispute is within the Hanafi school, not cross-madhhab.
4
Note the exception for father/guardian
For the orphan's property, all agree leasing for goods is permissible.
This indicates a narrower restriction applies only to waqf, not to minors' property.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
Later fatwa works follow Abu Hanifa's view for flexibility.
Ibn Nujaym includes the view without explicit rejection.

قوله: (فظاهره أن
Book of Waqf. Regarding his saying 'the judge leases it'... and it will come in the book of Ijarah that the ability to use in a corrupt [lease] is not sufficient, and it generally includes waqf. Al-Khassaf stated that if the administrator leases with a corrupt lease and the lessee is able to use but did not benefit in reality, then there is no rent upon him. In al-Zahiriyyah: It is permissible for the administrator to lease the waqf for merchandise according to Abu Hanifa, contrary to the two [Sahibayn]. The father and guardian if they lease a house for an orphan for merchandise, it is permissible without disagreement. In al-Qunya: It is not permissible for the administrator to purchase anything from the property of the mosque for himself, nor to sell to himself, even if there is an apparent benefit for the mosque, end. If you say: If the judge ordered something and [the administrator] did it, then it became clear that it is not legitimate or contains harm to the waqf, is the administrator liable? I say: In al-Qunya: The administrator asked the people of the neighborhood to lend from the mosque's property to the imam, and he refused, so the judge ordered him to do it, so he lent, then the imam died insolvent; the administrator is not liable, because the administrator is not allowed to lend the mosque's property. In Jami' al-Fusulayn: It is not for the administrator to deposit the property of waqf and mosque except with someone in his care, nor to lend it; if he lends it, he is liable, and likewise the borrower. And it is mentioned that if the administrator lends the mosque's property to take it back when needed, and it is safer than keeping it, there is no harm. And the administrator lending the surplus of the waqf revenue if it is kept safe. End. If you say: If the administrator is negligent in something of the waqf's interests, is he liable? I say: If it is in a specific item, he is liable; if it is in what is in the liability (dhimma), he is not liable. In al-Qunya: The mosque collapsed and the administrator did not preserve it until a beam was lost; he is liable. The administrator bought paint from a painter and deferred the price, then the painter became insolvent afterwards; he is not liable. End. In al-Bazzaziyyah: If the administrator refrains from collecting what is due from the guarantee contractors, he does not sin. If some of the contractors flee after a large amount of money has accumulated upon them due to the guarantee contract, the administrator is not liable. End. In al-Qunya: The administrator leased [the waqf], then was removed and another administrator was appointed. Some said the rent goes to the removed one, but the correct opinion is that it goes to the appointed one because the removed one leased it for the waqf, not for himself. If the administrator sold a house purchased with waqf property, he may cancel the sale with the buyer if the sale was not for more than the fair price. Similarly, if he is removed and another is appointed, the appointed one may cancel it without disagreement. If the judge gives permission to the administrator to mix waqf property with his own property as a facility for him, it is permissible and he is not liable. Likewise, if the judge mixes the property of a minor with his own. From Abu Yusuf: The guardian (wasi) if he mixes the minor's property with his own, he is not liable. The administrator may rescind the lease with the lessee before receiving the rent, and his rescission is effective upon the waqf; after receipt, not. If the administrator releases the lessee from the rent after completion of the term, the release is valid according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad. The administrator is liable for spending something from waqf property to scribes of fatwas and court minutes for the extraction of the waqf. If the administrator hires himself for work in the mosque and takes wages, it is not permissible in the apparent narration, and upon this is the fatwa. End. In Jami' al-Fusulayn: Since he is not valid as both employer and employee. And it is valid if the judge orders him to work in it. Then he said: In al-Qunya: The administrator became liable for waqf property by consuming it, then he spent the amount of the liability to the beneficiary without the judge's permission, he becomes free of liability. End. In al-Walwalijiyyah: The administrator may take a precautionary measure with waqf property on a person if he is wealthy, and if he takes a guarantor it is more beloved to me. In Jami' al-Fusulayn: The administrator has the authority to cancel a sale if it is better for the waqf. If you say: May the administrator spend the revenue of one year for a previous year? I say: No, because in al-Hawi al-Husayri and others: Abu Ja'far was asked about an administrator who collected revenue and distributed it among the beneficiaries of the waqf, but deprived one of them and did not give him, and spent his share on his own need. When the second year's revenue came out, the deprived one requested his share. Is he entitled to that? He said: If he wishes, he may hold the administrator liable, and if he wishes, he may follow his partners and share with them in what they took. If he chooses to hold the administrator liable, what they took is permissible for them, and he cannot take more than his share from this year's revenue. End. Its apparent implication is that if he chooses to follow the partners, he has no claim against the administrator, and the administrator does not give the deprived one anything from the second year's revenue, whether he chooses to hold him liable or to follow the partners. However, in al-Dhakhirah: If he chooses to follow the partners and share in what they took, he may take that from the shares of the partners in the second year because when he chose to follow the partners, it became clear that they took his share, so he may take from their shares similar to that in kind of his right. When he takes, they all have recourse against the administrator for what the administrator consumed from the deprived one's share, because he ensures the sultan of the revenue. The fatwa said al-Ramli and similarly if a wrongdoer seizes it and he cannot repel him except by spending his own money, he spends, as is known from the issue of the guardian when he covets the orphan's property and cannot repel him except by paying something from his property. Likewise, if he has nothing of the waqf property in his possession and such an issue occurs, and he borrows by order of the judge or seeks the judge's permission to pay that from his property to claim it back from the waqf property, as is known from the book of wasiyyah also. Reflect on his saying: (If he wishes, he may hold the administrator liable). Al-Ramli said: i.e., because he spent the share of others on his own need, so he became a transgressor. And his saying: 'If he wishes, he may follow his partners' i.e., because they took his share. His saying: (apparent implication that
Source pages p. 401

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq comments on the matn statement 'أجرها الحاكم' (the judge leases it) from Kanz al-Daqa'iq. The author, Ibn Nujaym, expands on several rulings related to the waqf administrator (qayyim/mutawalli).

First, he clarifies that if the administrator leases with a corrupt contract (fasidah) and the tenant is able to use the property but does not actually benefit, no rent is due. This is specific to waqf; in regular leases, mere ability to use may obligate rent, but here the text explicitly states it is insufficient.

Second, a key intra-Hanafi dispute: according to Abu Hanifa, it is permissible for the administrator to lease waqf property for a price in goods (يعرض) rather than cash, but Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (al-Shaybani) disagree. For the father or guardian leasing an orphan's property, there is no dispute—it is allowed. This difference stems from the nature of waqf as a perpetual trust; Abu Hanifa considers the benefit of flexibility, while the Sahibayn may see goods as less stable.

Third, the administrator cannot buy from the mosque's property for himself or sell to himself, even if it benefits the mosque, due to conflict of interest.

The passage then addresses several practical questions: (1) If the judge orders the administrator to do something that turns out invalid or harmful, the administrator is not liable because he acted under authority. A case from al-Qunya illustrates this: lending mosque money by judge's order, then the borrower dies insolvent; no liability on the administrator. (2) Negligence: if the administrator is negligent with a specific item (e.g., fails to preserve a beam), he is liable; but if the negligence is in something not physically in his custody (like a debt), he is not. (3) When the administrator is replaced, the rent from a lease concluded by the previous administrator goes to the new one, as the lease was for the waqf, not for the person. (4) The administrator may rescind a lease before receiving rent, but not after. He may also release (ibra) the lessee from rent after the term, valid according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad.

Further rulings: mixing waqf funds with personal funds is allowed only with judge's permission; the administrator may spend waqf money on fatwa scribes and court documents to protect the waqf; hiring oneself for mosque work is invalid as it conflates employer and employee; paying a wrongdoer to save waqf property may be allowed.

The final question about distributing revenue from one year for a previous year clarifies that the administrator cannot do so; if he misappropriates a beneficiary's share, the beneficiary can either hold the administrator liable or claim from the other partners who received the share. The Dhakhirah adds that if he claims from partners, they in turn recoup from the administrator.

Throughout, the passage emphasizes that the administrator is a trustee (amin) but becomes a guarantor (damin) if he transgresses.

Key Terms
القيم (al-qayyim): The administrator of a waqf or mosque, responsible for its management and maintenance.
المتولي (al-mutawalli): Same as al-qayyim; the person entrusted with managing the waqf.
العرض (al-'ard): Merchandise, goods, or any non-monetary commodity used as payment.
الإجارة الفاسدة (al-ijarah al-fasidah): A corrupt lease that fails to meet some condition for validity but is not void (batil). In waqf, such a lease does not obligate rent if the lessee did not actually benefit.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling on a corrupt lease (ijarah fasidah) of waqf property if the lessee did not benefit?
A) Rent is due because the lessee had the ability to use.
B) No rent is due because in waqf, actual benefit is required.
C) The lease is void and the administrator is liable.
D) The lease is valid and rent is due in cash.

Key Takeaway
In waqf, a corrupt lease does not obligate rent if no benefit was taken, and leasing for goods is valid by Abu Hanifa's preferred view.

Waqf — Endowment

12 min
The Waqf Administrator's Powers, Liabilities, and Revenue Distribution
Expansion on the duties and liabilities of the waqf administrator.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify key rules governing the waqf administrator's self-dealing prohibition, liability for negligence, mixing funds, rescission, and revenue distribution.

Core Idea
The waqf administrator is a trustee who becomes liable for specific physical assets but not for uncollected debts.

This lesson covers a range of practical rulings from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq on the administrator's duties and liabilities. Topics include his inability to buy/sell to himself, liability for negligence on specific items vs. debts, permissibility of mixing funds with judicial permission, rules on rescission and release, and the critical question of distributing revenue from one year to cover a previous year's shortfall.

Guided Path
1
Prohibited self-dealing
The administrator cannot buy from or sell to himself from mosque/waqf property, even if beneficial.
This prevents conflict of interest.
2
Liability for negligence
If negligence causes loss of a specific item (ayn), he is liable; if loss is in a debt (dhimma), he is not.
Example: losing a beam vs. tenant fleeing without paying.
3
Mixing funds with permission
With judge's permission, the administrator may mix waqf funds with his own, and he is not liable.
Abu Yusuf extends this to guardians.
4
Rescission and release
The administrator may rescind a lease before receiving rent; after receipt, he cannot. He may release the lessee from rent after the term.
Release is valid according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad.
5
Revenue distribution recourse
If the administrator wrongly withholds a beneficiary's share and spends it on himself, the beneficiary may hold him liable or claim from other beneficiaries who received excess. According to al-Dhakhirah, the latter can then recoup from the administrator.
This shows layered recourse.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الوقف عند قوله «أجرها الحاكم، وسيأتي في كتاب الإجارات أن التمكن في الفاسدة لا يكفي وهو بعمومه يتناول الوقف. وقد صرح الخصاف بأن المتولي إذا أجره إجارة فاسدة وتمكن المستأجر ولم ينتفع حقيقة فإنه لا الإجارة مع المستأجر قبل قبض الأجر وينفذ فسخه على الوقف وبعد القبض لا. ولو أبرأ القيم المستأجر عن الأجرة بعد تمام المدة تصح البراءة عند أبي حنيفة ومحمد، ويضمن للقيم صرف شيء من مال الوقف إلى كتبة الفتوى Third, the administrator cannot buy from the mosque's property for himself or sell to himself, even if it benefits the mosque, due to conflict of interest.

The Dhakhirah adds that if he claims from partners, they in turn recoup from the administrator.

المتولي (al-mutawalli): Same as al-qayyim; the person entrusted with managing the waqf.
العرض (al-'ard): Merchandise, goods, or any non-monetary commodity used as payment.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling if the administrator mixes waqf funds with his own without the judge's permission?
A) It is permissible and he is not liable.
B) It is a transgression and he is liable for losses.
C) It is permissible only if the funds are small.
D) It is prohibited regardless of permission.

Key Takeaway
The waqf administrator is liable for loss of specific assets but not for debts, and his powers are bounded by strict rules against self-dealing and unauthorized spending.
